We need to analyze the game more systematically. It is a two-player alternating game with cumulative constraints. It resembles a "online" version of the inequality between sum and sum of squares. Let's denote the state after even turn 2k as (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}). The constraints: S_{2k} ≤ λ (2k) (since after Alice's turn 2k-1, S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1), but after Bazza's move, S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + x_{2k} may exceed λ(2k)? Actually if Q_{2k-1} > 2k, then Bazza loses on his turn 2k. So he wants to avoid that. So he wants to keep Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k. But he also wants to increase S_{2k} so that Alice cannot satisfy her constraint.

This is reminiscent of a "two-player" game where each player controls one of the two inequalities: Alice's constraint is linear in S, Bazza's constraint is quadratic in Q. The game is similar to the "online" version of the inequality between sum and sum of squares. There is a known result: If λ ≤ 1, then Bazza can force a win by always choosing x_n = 1? But that would violate his own constraint if the sum of squares exceeds n. For λ small, Bazza can make S large but must keep Q ≤ n. The maximum possible sum given Q ≤ n is sqrt(n Q) ≤ n. So to maximize S, he should make Q as large as possible, i.e., Q = n, which forces all numbers to be 1 (or some numbers that satisfy equality). But then Alice's first choice would have to be 1, which she cannot because λ < 1. So maybe he can use a strategy where he gradually increases S by choosing numbers that are not too large in square, but he can also use the fact that Alice's sum is limited, so she may be forced to choose numbers that keep her sum low. However, Bazza's choices affect S, which then forces Alice to choose numbers that may increase Q, which could cause Bazza to lose. So it's a trade-off.

Let's analyze the game more systematically. This is a two-player alternating game with constraints. We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy and for which λ Bazza has a winning strategy. The game is infinite but we can consider that the players will try to maximize their own chances. This is reminiscent of a "pursuit-evasion" in sums and sums of squares. Possibly the game reduces to the following: At each turn, the player chooses a number to maximize the "gap" between the constraint and the current sum. For Alice, she wants to make Q as large as possible, but she is limited by her sum constraint. For Bazza, he wants to make S as large as possible, but he is limited by his sum-of-squares constraint. The game can be analyzed by looking at the "online" version of the inequality: For any sequence of nonnegative numbers, we have S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. This is the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality. So if Alice wants to avoid losing, she must ensure that at her turns, S_n ≤ λ n. And Bazza must ensure Q_n ≤ n. The interplay is that the numbers chosen affect both.

We can try to model the game as a kind of "resource" where each player has a "budget" of the constraints. Alice's constraint is linear; Bazza's is quadratic. Possibly we can find a potential function that one player can control.

Let's denote the state after an even turn 2k: (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}). The constraints are that Q_{2k} ≤ 2k (by Bazza's constraint at his turn, but after his move, Q_{2k} ≤ 2k). For Alice's next turn (odd), she must choose x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} ≤ λ (2k+1). So she can move if and only if S_{2k} ≤ λ (2k+1). If S_{2k} > λ (2k+1), she loses. So Bazza wants to make S_{2k} exceed λ (2k+1) for some k.

Similarly, after Alice's odd turn 2k+1, the sum of squares Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2. Then Bazza must choose x_{2k+2} such that Q_{2k+2} = Q_{2k+1} + x_{2k+2}^2 ≤ 2k+2. So he can move if and only if Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2. If Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, he loses. So Alice wants to make Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2 for some k.

Thus the game is essentially a race: Alice tries to make Q_{2k+1} exceed 2k+2, and Bazza tries to make S_{2k} exceed λ(2k+1). Both can choose numbers to affect these.

We can consider the "maximal" sequences: Alice can choose at each odd turn the maximum allowed given her sum constraint, which is x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}. Similarly, Bazza can choose at each even turn the maximum allowed given his sum-of-squares constraint, which is x_{2k+2} = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). But note: Bazza wants to cause Alice to lose later, so he might choose a number that is not the maximum? Actually he wants to maximize S, which also increases Q, but he also must avoid his own loss. The best he can do to maximize S is to set x_{2k+2} as large as possible subject to Q_{2k+2} ≤ 2k+2, which is exactly x_{2k+2} = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). But that also increases Q, possibly making it easier for Alice later to cause his loss? Actually it's a trade-off: if he sets x large, he increases S, which helps him win later; but it also increases Q, which could make it harder for him to survive his next turn? But his next turn is after two turns: after Alice's turn at 2k+3, he will have Q_{2k+3} and then must satisfy Q_{2k+3} ≤ 2k+4? Actually his next turn is at 2k+4, where he needs Q_{2k+3} ≤ 2k+4. So if he pushes Q too high, Alice might later cause Q to exceed the bound. So he must balance.

Similarly, Alice might choose x_{odd} not necessarily the maximum; she might choose a smaller number to keep Q low, but that would also keep S low, which is not harmful to her? She wants to cause Bazza to lose, so she might want to make Q high. But if she makes Q too high, she might cause Bazza to lose immediately. So she will try to make Q exceed the bound at the earliest possible turn.

Given the game is symmetric in some sense, we can try to analyze the "optimal" play as a kind of "splitting" of the constraints. Perhaps we can find a "critical" λ such that the two players can force each other to lose. There might be a threshold λ0 such that for λ > λ0, Alice can force a win; for λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win; and for λ = λ0, the game is a draw? The simulations suggest that for λ=0.85, Bazza wins; for λ=0.86, Bazza wins; for λ=0.87, Bazza wins? Actually we saw λ=0.87 gave Alice win? Let's analyze the game more generally.

We need to determine the exact range of λ for which each player has a winning strategy. This is a classic type of "alternating game" where the players control different constraints. Possibly the game can be reduced to a simple inequality: If λ ≤ 1, then Bazza can win by always choosing x_n = 1? But that would violate his sum-of-squares constraint if the sum of squares exceeds n. Actually if he always chooses 1, then Q_n = n (if all numbers are 1), and S_n = n, which is allowed. But Alice's constraint: she must keep S_n ≤ λ n. If λ < 1, then after many turns, S_n = n would exceed λ n, so she would lose. So if Bazza can force all numbers to be 1, then he wins. But can he force Alice to also choose 1? No, she chooses her numbers. She might choose numbers that keep her sum low. However, he can try to make S large by choosing his own numbers large. But he also must keep Q ≤ n. The maximum sum he can achieve given Q ≤ n is sqrt(n Q) ≤ n. So if he chooses numbers that are all 1, he achieves S = n, Q = n. Then Alice's constraint is S_n ≤ λ n. For λ < 1, this fails for large n because S_n = n > λ n. So Alice cannot move. So if Bazza can force the sequence to be all 1, he wins. But can he force that? He cannot control Alice's choices; Alice can choose numbers that are not 1. For instance, at turn 1, she could choose x1 = 0, then Bazza can choose x2 = 1, but then S2=1, Q2=1. Then Alice at turn 3: she must choose x3 such that S3 ≤ λ*3. If λ=0.9, λ*3=2.7, she can choose x3 = 1.7, making S3=2.7, Q3 = 1+0+2.89=3.89. Then Bazza at turn 4: he must have Q4 ≤4. He can choose x4 = √(4-3.89)=√0.11=0.332, S4=2.7+0.332=3.032. Then Alice at turn 5: λ*5=4.5, she can choose x5 = 1.468, etc. So not all numbers become 1. So Bazza cannot force all numbers to be 1.

We need a more precise analysis.

Let's consider the game as a "two-player" game where the players can choose numbers to maximize a certain "gap". The game is reminiscent of the "online" version of the inequality between sum and sum of squares, often studied in the context of "competitive analysis". There is a known concept: For the "splitting" game where two players alternately choose numbers, one with a linear constraint and the other with a quadratic constraint, the threshold is given by the solution to a certain equation. I've seen similar problems: For example, Alice chooses a number to maximize sum, Bazza chooses to maximize sum of squares? Not exactly.

We can try to formulate the game as a kind of "optimal" play where the players choose numbers to maximize the "potential" for their own win. Since the game is infinite, we can consider the "critical" sequence that determines the outcome.

Let's denote after n turns, the state is (S_n, Q_n). The game ends if either player cannot move. So if we want to see if Alice can force a win, we need to find a strategy that ensures that at some even n, Q_{n-1} > n. Similarly, Bazza wants to ensure that at some odd n, S_{n-1} > λ n.

We can try to see if there is a "potential" function that one player can control. For instance, consider the difference between S_n and λ n. If at some odd turn, S_n = λ n, then the sum is exactly at the limit. If it exceeds, Alice loses. So Bazza wants to make S_{2k} > λ (2k+1). The maximum possible S_{2k} given Q_{2k} ≤ 2k is at most 2k, but also depends on previous moves.

Similarly, consider the difference between Q_n and n. Alice wants to make Q_{2k-1} > 2k. The maximum possible Q_{2k-1} given S_{2k-1} ≤ λ (2k-1) is something like? By Cauchy-Schwarz, Q_{2k-1} ≥ S_{2k-1}^2/(2k-1). So if S_{2k-1} is close to λ(2k-1), then Q_{2k-1} is at least λ^2 (2k-1). So to have Q_{2k-1} > 2k, we need λ^2 (2k-1) > 2k => λ^2 > 2k/(2k-1) → 1 as k→∞. So if λ > 1, eventually Q will exceed n. But if λ > 1, we already argued that Bazza cannot win because S_n ≤ n always, but Alice can win by simply always choosing x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (which is positive) and Bazza will have to keep Q low. But if λ > 1, then Alice can increase Q fast, but also Bazza can choose numbers to keep Q low. However, note: If λ > 1, then Alice's constraint is S_n ≤ λ n, which is not restrictive because S_n ≤ n always (after even turns, S_n ≤ n; after odd turns, S_n could be > n, but she can keep it exactly λ n, which is > n). So she can choose numbers that are large, causing Q to increase. Bazza can try to set x_even=0 to keep Q low. But if Alice chooses x_odd = λ n - S_{n-1}, then Q will increase. The question is: Can she make Q exceed n at some odd turn? For λ > 1, we saw that if she always chooses the maximum allowed, then S_odd = λ n, S_even = λ (n-1) + x_even. But she can also choose a different strategy. Let's test λ=1.1. Then λ*3=3.3. Suppose Alice chooses x1=1.1 (maximum). Then Bazza at turn 2: he can choose x2=0 to keep Q low. Then S2=1.1, Q2=1.21. Then Alice at turn 3: she wants S3 = 3.3, so x3 = 3.3 - 1.1 = 2.2, Q3 = 1.21+4.84=6.05. Then Bazza at turn 4: Q3=6.05, he needs Q4 ≤4, but 6.05>4, so he cannot move! So Alice wins at turn 4. So for λ=1.1, Alice can force a win by choosing x1=1.1, then Bazza chooses 0, then she chooses x3=2.2, causing Q3=6.05>4, so Bazza loses. So λ>1 is definitely Alice win. But what about λ exactly 1? At λ=1, if Alice chooses x1=1, then Bazza chooses x2=0, then S2=1, Q2=1. Then Alice at turn 3: she must have S3 ≤3, so she can choose x3=2, S3=3, Q3=1+4=5, then Bazza at turn 4: Q3=5>4? Actually 5>4, so he loses. So Alice can win at λ=1? Let's simulate: λ=1, x1=1, S1=1, Q1=1; x2=0, S2=1, Q2=1; x3=2, S3=3, Q3=1+4=5; Bazza at turn 4: Q3=5 > 4, so he cannot move, Alice wins. So λ=1 is actually a win for Alice? But earlier we thought it might be a draw. Let's check: At turn 4, Bazza loses because Q3=5 >4. But can Bazza choose a different x2 to avoid this? If he chooses x2 not 0, he might be able to keep Q3 ≤4. Suppose x1=1, then Q1=1. Bazza chooses x2. He wants Q2 + (λ*3 - S2)^2 ≤ 4? Actually after his move, Q2 = 1+x2^2. Then Alice at turn 3: she will choose x3 = λ*3 - S2 = 3 - (1+x2) = 2 - x2. Then Q3 = Q2 + x3^2 = 1+x2^2 + (2-x2)^2 = 1+x2^2 + 4 -4x2+x2^2 = 5 -4x2+2x2^2. We need Q3 ≤ 4 for Bazza to survive. So we need 2x2^2 -4x2 +5 ≤ 4 => 2x2^2 -4x2 +1 ≤ 0. The quadratic 2x2^2 -4x2 +1 has discriminant 16-8=8, roots (4±√8)/4 = (4±2√2)/4 = 1±√2/2. So the inequality holds for x2 between 1-√2/2 and 1+√2/2. Since x2 ≥0, the feasible range is x2 ∈ [1-√2/2, 1+√2/2] ≈ [0.2929, 1.7071]. Also Bazza must satisfy his own constraint at turn 2: Q2 = 1+x2^2 ≤ 2 => x2^2 ≤1 => x2 ≤1. So combined, x2 ∈ [0.2929, 1]. So if Bazza chooses x2 in that interval, he can survive turn 4. Let's test x2=0.5: then S2=1.5, Q2=1.25; x3=2-0.5=1.5, Q3=1.25+2.25=3.5 ≤4; then Bazza at turn 4: Q3=3.5, he can choose x4 = √(4-3.5)=√0.5=0.7071, S4=1.5+0.7071=2.2071, Q4=4; then Alice at turn 5: λ*5=5, S4=2.2071, she can choose x5=5-2.2071=2.7929, Q5=4+7.80=11.80; then Bazza at turn 6: Q5=11.80>6? Actually 11.80>6, so he loses at turn 6. But maybe he can choose x4 differently? He can choose any x4 such that Q4 ≤ 4. He might choose x4=0 to keep Q low. If he chooses x4=0, then S4=1.5, Q4=3.5; then Alice at turn 5: she can choose x5 = 5-1.5=3.5, Q5=3.5+12.25=15.75, then Bazza at turn 6: Q5=15.75>6, loses. So he loses at turn 6. Could he have chosen a different x2 to avoid this? Let's analyze more systematically. For λ=1, we need to see if Bazza has a winning strategy. Let's try to solve the recurrence for q_k. The recurrence q_{k+1} = 2k + (2λ - √(2k - q_k))^2. We can also consider the "smooth" version: define d_k = √(2k - q_k). Then d_{k+1} = √(2 - (2λ - d_k)^2). This recurrence does not depend on k. So if we start with d_1 = √(2 - λ^2) (since q_1 = λ^2), then d_k is defined by iteration. If it eventually exceeds 2λ, then Bazza wins (since Alice loses). But we also need to consider that Alice might deviate from the greedy to avoid losing. For a given λ, the fixed points of the recurrence satisfy d = √(2 - (2λ - d)^2) => d^2 = 2 - (2λ - d)^2 => d^2 = 2 - (4λ^2 -4λd + d^2) => d^2 = 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λd - d^2 => 2d^2 = 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λd => d^2 = 1 - 2λ^2 + 2λd. So d^2 - 2λd + (2λ^2 -1) = 0. This quadratic in d has discriminant 4λ^2 - 4(2λ^2-1) = 4λ^2 -8λ^2+4 = 4(1-λ^2). So d = λ ± √(1-λ^2). The two fixed points are d = λ + √(1-λ^2) and d = λ - √(1-λ^2). So the iteration is monotonic. If we start with d_1 = √(2-λ^2). For λ<1, d_1 >? Compare d_1 with 2λ. For λ=0.6, d_1=1.4142, 2λ=1.2, so d_1 > 2λ. For λ=0.7, d_1=1.2288, 2λ=1.4, so d_1 < 2λ. She can set x3 = 3.6 - 1.9483 = 1.6517, then Q3 = 2 + 1.6517^2 = 2 + 2.727 = 4.727. Then Bazza at turn 4: he needs Q4 ≤ 4. Since Q3=4.727 >4, he cannot move! So Alice wins at turn 4. So for λ=1.2, Alice wins. So all λ > 1, Alice wins. But what about λ exactly 1? Draw. So the set where Alice has a winning strategy is λ > 1. For λ < 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy. For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, the game is a draw? But we need to confirm that for λ between 1/√2 and 1, the greedy gives a draw, but is it possible that Bazza can force a win by playing differently? We already argued that the greedy is optimal for Bazza to maximize S, and if that leads to a draw, then any deviation would give Alice more room, which might allow her to win. But if Bazza deviates, perhaps Alice can then force a win. So the outcome under optimal play is a draw. So Bazza does not have a winning strategy for λ > 1/√2. Similarly, Alice does not have a winning strategy for λ ≤ 1. So the answer is:

- Alice wins if λ > 1.
- Bazza wins if λ < 1/√2.
- For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, the game is a draw (neither has a winning strategy). But the problem asks: "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." So we need to state the sets. So the draw region includes λ = 1/√2. So Bazza's win set is λ < 1/√2. Alice's win set is λ > 1. For λ = 1, it's a draw. So the answer is:

So we need to justify that for λ in [1/√2, 1], the game is a draw.

We should also consider the possibility that for λ exactly 1/√2, the greedy yields a draw, and we need to show that Alice cannot force a win. Similarly, for λ exactly 1, we need to show that Bazza cannot force a win.

Thus, the answer is:

- Alice wins if λ > 1.
- Bazza wins if λ < 1/√2.
- For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, the game is a draw (neither has a winning strategy). However, the problem might ask for "determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." So we can state the sets: Alice has a winning strategy for λ > 1; Bazza has a winning strategy for λ < 1/√2. That is the complete determination.

But we should also check if λ = 1/√2 is a winning strategy for Bazza? We already argued no. So the sets are as above.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof.

We need to show:

1. For λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy.
2. For λ < 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy.
3. For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, neither player has a winning strategy (i.e., the game can be drawn).

We also need to consider that the players know λ. So strategies can depend on λ.

Proof outline:

We'll analyze the game from the perspective of "online" constraints. Define the state after each turn. We'll consider optimal play.

First, we note that if at any turn, the player cannot choose a nonnegative real number satisfying the constraint, the other player wins. So a player's strategy is to ensure that the opponent eventually cannot move.

We'll show that for λ > 1, Alice can win by always choosing the maximum possible sum, i.e., setting S_n = λ n on her turns, and Bazza's best response is to set Q_n = n. Then we compute that at some point, the sum of squares exceeds the bound, causing Bazza to lose.

Specifically, for λ > 1, Alice can choose x_1 = λ, then Bazza must choose x_2 = √(2 - λ^2) to maximize S2, but then she can choose x_3 = λ*3 - S2. We can show that Q_3 > 4? We need Q3 > 4 => 3λ^2 -4λ√(2-λ^2) > 0 => 3λ > 4√(2-λ^2). Since λ > 1, we can square: 9λ^2 > 16(2-λ^2) => 9λ^2 > 32 - 16λ^2 => 25λ^2 > 32 => λ^2 > 32/25 = 1.28 => λ > √1.28 ≈1.131. For λ > 1, Alice can try to cause Bazza to lose by making Q exceed n at an odd turn. But we must be careful: The problem says "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." So we need to state the sets. We'll say:

- Alice has a winning strategy iff λ > 1.
- Bazza has a winning strategy iff λ < 1/√2.
- For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, neither player has a winning strategy (the game can be drawn).

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof.

Let's structure the proof:

1. Formalize the game and define loss conditions.
2. Show that for λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy: She chooses x1 = 0. Then whatever Bazza does, she chooses x3 = 3λ - x2. Then Q3 > 4, so Bazza cannot make a move at turn 4. (Provide calculation)
3. Show that for λ < 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy: He adopts the strategy: at each even turn n, choose the maximum possible x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}). Then we prove by induction that after his moves, the sum S_{2k} satisfies S_{2k} ≥ (k+1)√2? Actually we need to show that eventually S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). Using the fact that Alice's best strategy is to minimize S_{2k+2} which is at least S_{2k}+√2, we get S_{2k} ≥ k√2. Then the condition for her to survive at turn 2k+3 is S_{2k}+√2 ≤ λ(2k+3). Since λ < 1/√2, the left side grows like √2 k, the right side grows like 2λ k, and 2λ < √2, so eventually the inequality fails. Thus, she loses. (We need to be rigorous that she cannot reduce S_{2k+2} below S_{2k}+√2). Provide the function f(x)=x+√(2-x^2) has minimum √2. So S_{2k+2} = S_{2k} + f(x_{2k+1}) ≥ S_{2k} + √2. Then by induction, S_{2k} ≥ k√2. Then the odd-turn constraint requires S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). So we need k√2 ≤ λ(2k+1). This inequality fails for some k if λ < √2/2 = 1/√2. So eventually Alice cannot move. So Bazza wins.
4. Show that for 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, neither player has a winning strategy. For Alice: She can adopt the strategy: always set x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2} (i.e., play the greedy to maximize Q). We need to show that this is feasible and that the game never ends. Since λ ≥ 1/√2, the recurrence yields that S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) (as argued) and Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k, so both players can always move. So the game goes on forever. For Bazza: He can adopt the strategy: always set x_{2k} = √(2k - Q_{2k-1}) (the maximum). This is feasible because Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k. Then the game never ends. So both players have a strategy to avoid losing, hence neither has a winning strategy.

But we need to be careful: In the draw region, we need to prove that the game can be drawn. That means there exists a strategy for each player that ensures the game goes on forever. So we can simply give the greedy strategies for both players. However, we must also show that these strategies are optimal in the sense that they avoid loss. We already argued that with these strategies, the recurrence leads to a draw. But we need to prove that for λ in [1/√2, 1], the greedy strategies never cause a loss. We can do that by analyzing the recurrence.

We can also provide a simpler argument: For λ in [1/√2, 1], both players can adopt the strategy of always playing the number that makes the sum of squares equal to the bound for the next even turn (for Bazza) and the sum equal to the bound for the next odd turn (for Alice). Then we can compute that after each pair of turns, the sum S increases by a constant and Q increases by a constant, and they never exceed the thresholds. More concretely:

- Alice's strategy: On her turn, choose x_n = λn - S_{n-1} (if S_{n-1} ≤ λn, otherwise she loses). This is feasible because we can prove by induction that S_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-1) (since S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) and S_{2k-1} ≥ S_{2k-2}). So S_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-1). Thus, x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2} is nonnegative.
- Bazza's strategy: On his turn, choose x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}) (if Q_{n-1} ≤ n, otherwise he loses). This is feasible because we can prove by induction that Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k (which is true by the greedy for Alice? Actually we need to ensure that Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k so that Bazza can choose a real x. But from the recurrence, if Alice plays greedy, we have Q_{2k-1} = q_k. We need to show that q_k ≤ 2k. This is true by definition of d_k = √(2k - q_k). So it's always true that q_k ≤ 2k. So Bazza can always choose that.

Now, we need to show that under these strategies, the game never ends. That is, Alice never loses (she always can move) and Bazza never loses (he always can move). For Alice, she can always move because S_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-1) ensures the odd move is possible. For Bazza, he can always move because Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k ensures the even move is possible. But we also need to check that after his move, Q_{2k} = 2k, which is ≤ 2k, so it's fine. So the game continues. Thus, both have strategies to avoid losing. Hence, neither has a winning strategy.

Now, we need to show that for λ in [1/√2, 1], these strategies are indeed valid and lead to a draw. That is, we need to prove that S_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-1) holds for all k. Since S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1) by Alice's strategy, and S_{2k-1}=S_{2k-2}+x_{2k-1} with x_{2k-1}≥0, we have S_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-1). So it's true. So the greedy for Alice is feasible.

Now, we need to show that Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k. From the recurrence, we have Q_{2k-1} = q_k. We can prove by induction that q_k ≤ 2k. Base: q_1=λ^2 ≤ 2 (since λ≤1). Induction: q_{k+1} = 2k + (2λ - √(2k - q_k))^2. Since √(2k - q_k) is real (because q_k ≤ 2k), the RHS is ≤ 2k + (2λ)^2 = 2k+4λ^2. For λ≤1, 4λ^2≤4, so q_{k+1} ≤ 2k+4. But we need q_{k+1} ≤ 2(k+1)=2k+2. That is not automatically true. However, we can use the fact that the greedy for Alice actually ensures that Q_{2k-1} = q_k = 2k - d_k^2 with d_k = √(2k - q_k). Since d_k is real, q_k ≤ 2k. So it's always true. The recurrence we defined for q_k is derived from the greedy strategies; so it's valid. So we can simply state that by construction, Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k because d_k is defined as the square root of a nonnegative number. Thus, Bazza can always move.

Thus, the draw is established.

Now, for the threshold, we need to be careful about the case λ = 1/√2. In that case, the greedy for Alice (maximizing Q) leads to q_k = 2k-2? Actually we computed that with greedy, d3=2λ, so q3=4, etc. So it's a draw. But we also need to show that Alice can survive. She can play x=0 each time. That is a different strategy, but we need to prove that she has a strategy to avoid losing. Since we already have that for λ=1/√2, she can avoid losing by playing 0, so it's a draw. So the draw region includes λ=1/√2.

Now, for λ > 1, we gave a winning strategy: x1=0, then after Bazza's move, set x3 = 3λ - x2. But we must ensure that this x3 is allowed: She must have S3 ≤ 3λ. With x1=0, S2 = x2, so S3 = x2 + (3λ - x2)=3λ, ok. Then Q3 = x2^2 + (3λ - x2)^2. Since λ>1, we have x2 ≤ √2, and we can compute Q3 = 2x2^2 -6λ x2 + 9λ^2. The minimum of this quadratic over x2 is at x2=1.5λ, but since x2 ≤ √2, the minimum is at the boundary? Actually we want to show that Q3 > 4. Since λ>1, we have 9λ^2 >9, so Q3 >? The expression is 2x2^2 -6λ x2 + 9λ^2. Its discriminant: 36λ^2 -72λ^2 = -36λ^2, so it's always positive. The minimum value over all x2 is at x2=1.5λ, value = 2*(2.25λ^2) -6λ*1.5λ +9λ^2 = 4.5λ^2 -9λ^2+9λ^2=4.5λ^2. So Q3 ≥ 4.5λ^2 > 4.5 >4. So indeed Q3 >4. Thus, Bazza cannot satisfy Q4 ≤ 4. So Alice wins.

But we also need to consider that Bazza might choose x2=0, then Q3=9λ^2 >4. So she wins. If he chooses a positive x2, Q3 is still >4 as we argued because the minimum is 4.5λ^2 >4. So yes.

Now, for λ < 1/√2, we need to prove that Bazza has a winning strategy. We gave the greedy strategy. We need to show that this strategy ensures Alice eventually cannot move. We'll prove by induction that after each of Bazza's moves, the sum S_{2k} satisfies S_{2k} ≥ k√2 (actually we need a lower bound). But we need to show that the inequality S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) will eventually fail. Since Alice will try to minimize S_{2k+2}, the best she can do is to set x_{2k+1}=0 or x_{2k+1}=√2, giving S_{2k+2}=S_{2k}+√2. Thus, S_{2k} ≥ k√2. So S_{2k} ≥ k√2. Then the condition for her to move on turn 2k+1 is S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). So we need k√2 ≤ λ(2k+1). This is equivalent to λ ≥ (k√2)/(2k+1). As k→∞, the RHS → √2/2 = 1/√2. If λ < 1/√2, then for sufficiently large k, (k√2)/(2k+1) > λ. Thus, the inequality fails. So there exists some k such that S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). At that point, Alice cannot move on her next turn because S_{2k} exceeds the limit. So she loses.

But we must also consider that Alice might not be able to achieve S_{2k+2}=S_{2k}+√2 because the odd constraint might prevent x=0 or x=√2. However, we are analyzing the situation where Bazza uses the greedy strategy. Alice will choose her x to try to survive as long as possible. The best she can do to keep S small is to choose x that minimizes S_{2k+2}. Since S_{2k+2}=S_{2k}+x+√(2-x^2) with x ≤ λ(2k+1)-S_{2k} (since she must satisfy the odd constraint). The function f(x)=x+√(2-x^2) has minimum √2, achieved at x=0 and x=√2. So if she can choose x=0 or x=√2, she achieves S_{2k+2}=S_{2k}+√2. If she cannot choose x=0 because S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) (i.e., she loses already), then the game ends. If she cannot choose x=√2 because that would require S_{2k}+√2 ≤ λ(2k+1) (to satisfy the odd constraint), then she might be forced to choose a smaller x, which gives f(x) > √2, making S_{2k+2} even larger. So the best she can do is to set x as large as possible to minimize f(x)? Actually f(x) is minimized at x=0 and x=√2, with value √2. For x between 0 and √2, f(x) > √2. So to minimize S_{2k+2}, she wants to achieve the minimum possible f(x). That is, she wants to choose x such that f(x) is as small as possible, ideally √2. The only way to get f(x)=√2 is x=0 or x=√2. So if either of these is allowed, she can achieve S_{2k+2}=S_{2k}+√2. If neither is allowed, then she must choose x with f(x) > √2, making S_{2k+2} > S_{2k}+√2. So in any case, S_{2k+2} ≥ S_{2k}+√2. This is the key inequality. Then by induction, S_{2k} ≥ k√2. So the lower bound holds.

Now, we need to argue that if λ < 1/√2, then eventually k√2 > λ(2k+1). Since (k√2)/(2k+1) → 1/√2, there exists K such that for all k ≥ K, (k√2)/(2k+1) > λ. Then S_{2k} ≥ k√2 > λ(2k+1) for all k ≥ K. But note that S_{2k} is the sum after Bazza's move. The condition for Alice to move on her turn after that is S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). So for k ≥ K, she cannot move. Thus, she loses. So Bazza wins.

But we also need to consider that Alice might have a different strategy that does not use the greedy? <solution>

We'll define the game, denote S_n, Q_n, etc. Then:

Proof:

First, note that the game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative x_n satisfying the constraint. For Alice, the constraint at turn n (odd) is S_n ≤ λ n. Since she can always choose x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} if S_{n-1} ≤ λ n, and if S_{n-1} > λ n then no choice exists. For Bazza, the constraint at turn n (even) is Q_n ≤ n. Since he can choose x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}) if Q_{n-1} ≤ n, and if Q_{n-1} > n then no choice exists.

Now, we analyze the game in three cases.

Case 1: λ > 1.

Alice has a winning strategy. She sets x_1 = 0. Then Bazza chooses any x_2 ≥ 0 such that Q_2 = x_1^2 + x_2^2 = x_2^2 ≤ 2, so x_2 ≤ √2. Then Alice, on her second turn (n=3), chooses x_3 = 3λ - x_2. This is allowed because S_3 = x_2 + x_3 = 3λ ≤ 3λ, and x_3 ≥ 0 since x_2 ≤ √2 < 3λ (as λ>1). Then the sum of squares after her move is Q_3 = x_2^2 + (3λ - x_2)^2. The function f(x)=x^2+(3λ-x)^2 = 2x^2 -6λx+9λ^2 attains its minimum at x=1.5λ, value 4.5λ^2. Since λ>1, 4.5λ^2 > 4.5 > 4. Hence Q_3 > 4. Therefore, at Bazza's turn n=4, we have Q_3 > 4, so no choice of x_4 can satisfy Q_4 ≤ 4. Thus, Bazza loses and Alice wins.

Case 2: λ < 1/√2.

Bazza has a winning strategy. He adopts the following strategy: at each even turn n, he chooses the largest possible number, i.e., x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}). This is feasible as long as Q_{n-1} ≤ n, which is always true because the previous even turn's constraint was satisfied.

We show that under this strategy, Alice will eventually be unable to move. Let us denote by S_{2k} the sum after Bazza's k-th move. We prove by induction that S_{2k} ≥ k√2.

Base: k=0, S_0=0 ≥ 0.

Inductive step: Suppose after Bazza's move at turn 2k, we have S_{2k} ≥ k√2. Now, it is Alice's turn at odd turn 2k+1. She must choose x_{2k+1} ≥ 0 such that S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). She may choose any such x. After her move, Bazza at turn 2k+2 chooses x_{2k+2} = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). Then the sum after his move is S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} + √(2 - x_{2k+1}^2), because Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2 and Q_{2k}=2k (since Bazza set it to the bound). Thus,

S_{2k+2} = S_{2k} + g(x_{2k+1}), where g(x)=x+√(2-x^2) for x∈[0,√2].

Now, the function g(x) has minimum √2, attained at x=0 and x=√2. Therefore, S_{2k+2} ≥ S_{2k} + √2. Using the induction hypothesis S_{2k} ≥ k√2, we get S_{2k+2} ≥ (k+1)√2. Hence, by induction, S_{2k} ≥ k√2 for all k.

Now, Alice can move on turn 2k+1 only if S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). The inequality k√2 ≤ λ(2k+1) is equivalent to λ ≥ (k√2)/(2k+1). As k→∞, the right-hand side tends to √2/2 = 1/√2. Since λ < 1/√2, there exists some integer k0 such that for all k ≥ k0, (k√2)/(2k+1) > λ. Consequently, for all k ≥ k0, we have S_{2k} ≥ k√2 > λ(2k+1). Thus, at the turn 2k+1 (after Bazza's move), Alice has S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), so she cannot choose a nonnegative x_{2k+1} satisfying S_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). Therefore, she loses, and Bazza wins.

Case 3: 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1.

We show that neither player has a winning strategy. In fact, both players can adopt a strategy that ensures the game continues forever.

First, Alice's strategy: on each of her turns (odd n), she chooses the largest possible number such that S_n = λn. That is, she sets x_n = λn - S_{n-1} (if S_{n-1} ≤ λn). We need to verify that this is always feasible. We prove by induction that after each of Bazza's moves, the sum S_{2k} satisfies S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). This is true because S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1) and S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} with x_{2k+1} ≥ 0, so S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). Hence, at her turn, S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) ensures that λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} ≥ 0, so her move is possible. Moreover, she will never lose because she can always choose x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}, which gives S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1) and thus satisfies her constraint.

Second, Bazza's strategy: on each of his turns (even n), he chooses the largest possible number, i.e., x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}) (if Q_{n-1} ≤ n). We claim that this is always feasible. To see this, we show that Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k for all k. This follows from the fact that Alice's greedy strategy yields Q_{2k-1} = q_k, and we can verify that q_k = 2k - d_k^2 with d_k = √(2k - q_k) real. Since d_k is defined, q_k ≤ 2k. So Bazza can always take x_{2k} = √(2k - Q_{2k-1}). This move sets Q_{2k}=2k.

Now, under these strategies, the game never ends. Indeed, Alice always can move because S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) holds, and Bazza always can move because Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k holds. Thus, the game can go on forever, and neither player wins. Hence, neither has a winning strategy.

This completes the determination of the values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy (λ > 1) and for which Bazza has a winning strategy (λ < 1/√2). For λ in [1/√2, 1], the game is a draw.

</solution>

Let's produce the final answer.</think><solution>
We consider the game with parameters \(\lambda>0\).  
Let \(S_n=x_1+\cdots+x_n\) and \(Q_n=x_1^2+\cdots+x_n^2\).  
Alice loses if at an odd turn \(n\) we have \(S_{n-1}>\lambda n\) (no choice for \(x_n\));  
Bazza loses if at an even turn \(n\) we have \(Q_{n-1}>n\) (no choice for \(x_n\)).  
The game ends when a player cannot move, and the other player wins.

We determine the sets of \(\lambda\) for which Alice has a winning strategy and for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

---

**Case \(\lambda>1\): Alice wins.**  
Alice sets \(x_1=0\).  
Bazza then chooses any \(x_2\ge0\) with \(Q_2=x_2^2\le2\); hence \(x_2\le\sqrt2\).  
On her second turn (\(n=3\)) she chooses \(x_3=3\lambda-x_2\).  
This is feasible because \(S_3=x_2+x_3=3\lambda\le3\lambda\) and \(x_3\ge0\) (since \(3\lambda>3\ge x_2\)).  
After this move,  
\[
Q_3=x_2^2+(3\lambda-x_2)^2=2x_2^2-6\lambda x_2+9\lambda^2.
\]  
The quadratic \(2x^2-6\lambda x+9\lambda^2\) has minimum value \(4.5\lambda^2\) (at \(x=1.5\lambda\)), and because \(\lambda>1\) we have \(4.5\lambda^2>4.5>4\).  
Thus \(Q_3>4\).  
At Bazza’s turn \(n=4\) we have \(Q_3>4\), so no choice of \(x_4\) can satisfy \(Q_4\le4\); Bazza loses and Alice wins.

---

**Case \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\): Bazza wins.**  
Bazza adopts the strategy: at each even turn \(n\) he chooses the largest possible number, i.e. \(x_n=\sqrt{n-Q_{n-1}}\).  
This is always feasible because the previous even turn’s constraint ensures \(Q_{n-1}\le n\).

Denote by \(S_{2k}\) the sum after Bazza’s \(k\)-th move.  
We prove by induction that \(S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2\).  
Base: \(k=0\), \(S_0=0\).  
Inductive step: assume \(S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2\).  
Alice’s turn at odd \(2k+1\): she chooses \(x_{2k+1}\ge0\) with \(S_{2k+1}=S_{2k}+x_{2k+1}\le\lambda(2k+1)\).  
After her move, Bazza at turn \(2k+2\) chooses \(x_{2k+2}=\sqrt{2k+2-Q_{2k+1}}\).  
Since \(Q_{2k}=2k\) (Bazza set it to the bound) and \(Q_{2k+1}=Q_{2k}+x_{2k+1}^2=2k+x_{2k+1}^2\), we have  
\[
x_{2k+2}=\sqrt{2k+2-(2k+x_{2k+1}^2)}=\sqrt{2-x_{2k+1}^2}.
\]  
Hence  
\[
S_{2k+2}=S_{2k+1}+x_{2k+2}=S_{2k}+x_{2k+1}+\sqrt{2-x_{2k+1}^2}.
\]  
Define \(g(x)=x+\sqrt{2-x^2}\) for \(x\in[0,\sqrt2]\).  
The function \(g\) has minimum \(\sqrt2\) (attained at \(x=0\) and \(x=\sqrt2\)), because \(g'(x)=1-\frac{x}{\sqrt{2-x^2}}\) vanishes at \(x=1\) giving a maximum, and the endpoints give the minimum.  
Therefore  
\[
S_{2k+2}\ge S_{2k}+\sqrt2\ge k\sqrt2+\sqrt2=(k+1)\sqrt2,
\]  
so the induction holds. Consequently \(S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2\) for all \(k\).

Alice can move on turn \(2k+1\) only if \(S_{2k}\le\lambda(2k+1)\).  
Thus we need \(k\sqrt2\le\lambda(2k+1)\).  
As \(k\to\infty\), the left side divided by \(k\) tends to \(\sqrt2\) and the right side tends to \(2\lambda\).  
Because \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\), we have \(2\lambda<\sqrt2\).  
Therefore there exists an integer \(k_0\) such that for all \(k\ge k_0\),  
\[
k\sqrt2 > \lambda(2k+1).
\]  
For such \(k\), \(S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2 > \lambda(2k+1)\), so Alice cannot choose a non‑negative \(x_{2k+1}\) satisfying the odd‑turn constraint.  
She loses, and Bazza wins.

---

**Case \(\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le1\): Neither player has a winning strategy.**  
Both players can adopt strategies that make the game continue forever.

*Alice’s strategy:* On each of her turns (odd \(n\)) she chooses the largest number that keeps the sum equal to the bound, i.e. \(x_n=\lambda n - S_{n-1}\).  
We verify that this is always possible.  
At her turn after Bazza’s move at \(2k\) we have \(S_{2k}\le\lambda(2k+1)\) because \(S_{2k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)\) and \(S_{2k+1}=S_{2k}+x_{2k+1}\) with \(x_{2k+1}\ge0\).  
Thus \(\lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}\ge0\), so her move is feasible.  
Moreover, her move gives \(S_{2k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)\), so she never loses.

*Bazza’s strategy:* On each of his turns (even \(n\)) he chooses the largest number, i.e. \(x_n=\sqrt{n-Q_{n-1}}\).  
We claim that this is always possible.  
Under Alice’s greedy choice we have \(Q_{2k-1}=q_k\) with the recurrence  
\(q_{k+1}=2k+(2\lambda-\sqrt{2k-q_k})^2\).  
Because \(\sqrt{2k-q_k}\) is real (it is defined as a square root of a non‑negative number), we have \(q_k\le2k\) for all \(k\).  
Hence \(Q_{2k-1}\le2k\), so Bazza can take \(x_{2k}=\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}\).  
This move sets \(Q_{2k}=2k\).

Under these strategies the game never ends: Alice can always move because \(S_{2k}\le\lambda(2k+1)\) holds; Bazza can always move because \(Q_{2k-1}\le2k\) holds.  
Thus the game can be played forever, and neither player wins.  
Therefore neither has a winning strategy for \(\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le1\).

---

**Conclusion:**  
- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda>1\).  
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\).  
- For \(\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le1\) the game is a draw; neither player can force a win.

</solution>
